            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/aenm.202405803')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '165ccf37-166a-4f3f-be7d-80556789ccb2'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <h1 class="citation__title">Modulating Double-Layer Solvation Structure via Dual-Weak-Interaction for Stable Sodium-Metal Batteries</h1>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="publicaitonContent-versions"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   <div class="loa comma visible-xs mobile-authors loa-authors-trunc">
      <div class="comma__list"><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Shi/Tianze" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="am1" data-db-target-for="am1" aria-controls="am1" aria-haspopup="true" id="am1_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Tianze Shi</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="am1" aria-labelledby="am1_Ctrl" role="region" id="am1">

               <p>School of Chemistry and Chemical Engineering, Nanjing University, Nanjing, 210093 China</p>
               <p>College of Engineering and Applied Sciences, Jiangsu Key Laboratory of Artificial Functional Materials, National Laboratory of Solid State Microstructures, Collaborative Innovation Centre of Advanced Microstructures, Center of Energy Storage Materials &amp; Technology, Nanjing University, Nanjing, 210093 China</p>
               <p>Lab of Power and Energy Storage Batteries, Shenzhen Research Institute of Nanjing University, Shenzhen, 518000 China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Shi/Tianze">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Hou/Ruilin" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="am2" data-db-target-for="am2" aria-controls="am2" aria-haspopup="true" id="am2_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Ruilin Hou</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="am2" aria-labelledby="am2_Ctrl" role="region" id="am2">
               <p class="author-name">Ruilin Hou</p>
               <p>Lab of Power and Energy Storage Batteries, Shenzhen Research Institute of Nanjing University, Shenzhen, 518000 China</p>
               <p>College of Engineering and Applied Sciences, Jiangsu Key Laboratory of Artificial Functional Materials, National Laboratory of Solid State Microstructures, Collaborative Innovation Centre of Advanced Microstructures, Center of Energy Storage Materials &amp; Technology, Nanjing University, Nanjing, 210093 China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Hou/Ruilin">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Zheng/Linlin" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="am3" data-db-target-for="am3" aria-controls="am3" aria-haspopup="true" id="am3_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Linlin Zheng</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="am3" aria-labelledby="am3_Ctrl" role="region" id="am3">

src="/specs/products/acropolis/pericles/releasedAssets/images/pdf-icon-169a2eb30e52100e76dfa5f4b66998e6.png" alt="">PDF</a></div>



        
        <div class="relative"><a href="#" data-db-target-for="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865" data-db-switch="icon-close_thin" aria-haspopup="true" aria-controls="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865_Pop" role="button" id="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865_Ctrl" aria-label="Tools" class="article-tools__ctrl"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Tools"></i><span>Tools</span></a><div data-db-target-of="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865" aria-labelledby="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865_Ctrl" id="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865_Pop" class="article-tools__block fixed dropBlock__holder"><ul role="menu" class="rlist w-slide--list"><li role="presentation" class="article-tool"><a href="https://advanced.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/action/rightsLink?doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405803&amp;mode=" role="menuitem" target="_blank"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-permissions-key"></i><span>Request permission</span></a></li><li role="presentation" class="article-tool"><a href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405803" role="menuitem" target="_self"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-export-icon"></i><span>Export citation</span></a></li><li role="presentation" class="article-tool"><a href="/personalize/addFavoritePublication?doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405803" role="menuitem" target="_self"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-favorites"></i><span>Add to favorites</span></a></li><li role="presentation" class="article-tool"><a href="/action/addCitationAlert?doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405803" role="menuitem" target="_self"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Track-citations"></i><span>Track citation</span></a></li></ul></div></div>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d110631300" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-metal batteries are the most promising low-cost and high-energy-density new energy storage technology. However, the sodium-metal anode has poor reversibility, which can be optimized by constructing the robust solid electrolyte interphase (SEI). Here, a concept of dual-weak-interaction electrolyte (DWIE) is demonstrated, its double-layer solvation structure is composed of weakly solvated tetrahydrofuran as the inner layer, and dipole interaction are introduced in the outer layer by dibutyl ether. This double-layer solvation structure dominated by contact ion pairs and aggregates can promote to deriving of inorganic-rich SEI film, resulting in smooth and dendrite-free sodium-metal deposition. By adjusting the molecular configuration of dibutyl ether to diisobutyl ether, the dipole interaction is further enhanced, resulting in stronger weakly solvating effect. Thus, the Na||Cu cells using the optimized DWIE achieved a high Coulombic efficiency of 99.22%, surpassing most electrolyte design strategies. Meanwhile, at 5C, the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP)||Na cell achieves stable cycling exceeding 3000 cycles. Even under rigorous conditions of ≈8.8 mg cm<sup>−2</sup> NVP loading and 50 µm thickness Na, the full cell can achieve a long cycling lifespan of 217 cycles. The pioneering concept paves the way for crafting readily achievable, cost-effective, and eco-friendly electrolytes tailored for SMBs, and offers potential applications in other battery systems.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202405803-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202405803-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted continuous interest in recent years, since their acknowledged resource sustainability, wide operation temperature range, and low cost, which endow SIBs with tremendous potential for applications in large-scale energy storage and extreme environmental conditions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0001_R_d110631211e461" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Soc. Rev. Chem. Soc. Rev. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Funct. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, due to inherent properties of electrode materials, the energy density of SIBs is low and limits its wider application. Utilizing a sodium-metal anode with a low redox potential (−2.71 V vs S.H.E.) and high specific capacity (1166 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) is the best choice for increasing the energy density of SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0002_R_d110631211e468" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Storage Mater. Nano Energy">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the continuous and uncontrollable electrochemical reactions between the high reactivity sodium-metal anode and electrolytes stand out as the critical challenge in the development of sodium-metal batteries (SMBs).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0003_R_d110631211e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Energy Environ. Sci. ACS Mater. Lett. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the repetitive formation and rupture of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) significantly impact the nucleation, deposition, and stripping of sodium (Na) on the electrode surface, causing the formation of “Na dendrites” and “dead Na”.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0004_R_d110631211e479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Ultimately, this process leads to irreversible Na inventory loss and poses a severe safety hazard.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0003_R_d110631211e484" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Energy Environ. Sci. ACS Mater. Lett. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Mater.">3</a>, <a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0005_R_d110631211e484" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nature">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, establishing a stable SEI is absolutely crucial for achieving high-performance and high-safety SMBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0006_R_d110631211e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Sci.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Abundant research indicates that the formation of a stable SEI is highly dependent on the solvation structure and electrolyte components.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0007_R_d110631211e497" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - ACS Energy Lett. ACS Nano Adv. Energy Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. EcoMat">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meticulously designing the solvation structure of the electrolyte to establish a stable SEI film is the most effective approach for enhancing the performance of SMBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0008_R_d110631211e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nat. Energy ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Energy Environ. Sci.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To this end, high-concentration electrolytes (HCE) and localized high-concentration electrolytes (LHCE),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0009_R_d110631211e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Chem. Commun. Chem. Eng. J. J. Power Sources ACS Appl. Energy Mater. ACS Energy Lett. ACS Energy Lett. J. Am. Chem. Soc.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> in which the solvation structures are dominant by the contact ion pairs (CIPs) and aggregates (AGGs), are developed to generating an anion-derived inorganic-rich SEI on the sodium-metal anode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0010_R_d110631211e513" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nat. Energy Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, the commercial viability of HCE and LHCE is hindered by their high salt concentration and the use of expensive diluents. It is worth noting that weakly solvating electrolytes (WSEs) offer a cost-effective solution while concurrently facilitating the formation of an anion-derived SEI.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0011_R_d110631211e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The key to designing WSEs lies in reducing the binding energy between cations and solvents, consequently enhancing the proportion of CIPs and AGGs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0012_R_d110631211e524" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> At present, WSEs design strategies include the following two approaches: on the one side, regulating the molecular structure of solvents to alter their physicochemical properties. Specifically, for linear solvents, shortening the middle alkyl chain to reduce chelation ability (e.g., dimethoxymethane (DMM)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0013_R_d110631211e529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>), increasing the terminal alkyl chain to enhance steric hindrance effects (e.g., 1,2-diethoxyethane (DEE),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0014_R_d110631211e534" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Small Small">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> diethoxy methane (DEM),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0015_R_d110631211e540" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> dipropyl ether (DPE)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0016_R_d110631211e545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nat. Commun.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>), and removing oxygen atoms to eliminate chelation ability (e.g., diethyl ether<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0017_R_d110631211e550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>), have been proven effective in reducing binding energy. For cyclic solvents, enlarging cyclic structure (e.g., tetrahydropyran (THP)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0018_R_d110631211e556" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Energy Lett. J. Am. Chem. Soc.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>) can enhance steric hindrance and electron-withdrawing effects, thereby altering their practical solvating strengths. On the other side, fluorinated solvents can not only achieve weakly solvating effect through the high electronegativity of fluorine but also broaden the electrochemical stability window (ESW) of the electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0019_R_d110631211e561" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Energy Storage Mater. Nat. Commun. Nat. Energy ACS Energy Lett.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Currently, the research predominantly focuses on the structural design of solvent molecules within single-solvent systems,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0020_R_d110631211e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nat. Energy Nature">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> that is selecting the solvents with inherent weakly solvating abilities to reduce the binding energy between cations and solvents. Consequently, the scope of solvents suitable for implementing the weak solvation strategy is significantly constrained. In addition, individual solvents still exhibit limitations in solubility, ionic conductivity, oxidative/reductive stability, and other factors that directly impact battery performance. Furthermore, the high cost and environmental hazards of fluorinated solvents make commercial applications challenging.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0021_R_d110631211e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hence, it is imperative to investigate universally applicable methods for implementing the weak solvation strategy and to develop versatile, fluorine-free solvent systems. Recently, an increasing number of innovative design concepts are providing novel insights into electrolyte design. For example, molecular-docking electrolytes<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0022_R_d110631211e578" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nat. Chem.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and cis-trans electrolytes,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0023_R_d110631211e584" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nat. Commun.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> they activate non coordinated solvents through hydrogen bond interaction and adjust solvent configuration through ambient temperature to solve the problem of salt dissociation, respectively. Additionally, Fan et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0024_R_d110631211e589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nat. Energy">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> modulated the oscillation amplitude of cations and anions at the electrode-electrolyte interface by adjusting the dielectric constant of the diluent, thereby enhancing the cations-anions coordination, which is known as “oscillatory solvation chemistry”. These innovative concepts highlight the feasibility of indirectly modulating cation-anion interactions by adjusting the chemical environment surrounding the primary solvation cluster, or even by varying temperature. They also inspire us to shift our focus beyond the primary solvation structure.</p>
         
         <p>Here, we present a novel dual-weak-interaction electrolyte (DWIE) design concept by manipulating the dipole interactions among different solvent molecules to further reduce the binding energy between cations and solvents, achieving an anion-deeply engaged solvation structure. In the designed electrolyte of 1 mol kg<sup>−1</sup> (m) NaFSI in THF/ dibutyl ether (DBE) (1:1 by vol) (hereafter denoted as 1-T/B), the THF with weak Na<sup>+</sup> coordinated ability serving as the main solvent exists in the primary solvation sheath while the cosolvent of DBE is hardly coordinated with Na<sup>+</sup>. The DBE in the outer layer of solvation sheath forms the dipole interactions with THF to further weaken the Na<sup>+</sup>-THF interactions, allowing more anions to enter the primary solvation sheath. Thereby, the solvation structure of the designed electrolyte is dominated by CIPs and AGGs, which promotes the formation of an anion-derived SEI enriched with inorganic components and significantly stabilizes the sodium-metal anode (Coulombic efficiency (CE): 98.67%). Further optimize the cosolvent molecular configuration by replacing DBE with diisobutyl ether (DBEi) to increase the dipole interactions and weaken the interactions of Na<sup>+</sup>-THF. Specifically, the CE of Na||Cu cell further enhances to 99.22%. Based on the optimized electrolyte, the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) ||Na full-cell displays a long cycling of exceeding 3000 cycles at 5C. Even using high-loading cathode (NVP, ≈8.8 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>) and 50 µm thickness Na, the full-cell retains more than 93.58% of the initial capacity over 210 cycles at 1C. This work opens up a viable pathway and introduces new perspectives for the design of electrolytes.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202405803-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202405803-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405803-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202405803-sec-0030-title">2.1 Design Strategies and Characterizations of the DWIEs</h3>
            
            <p>Typically, the ether (1, 2-dimethoxyethane (DME))-based electrolytes are commonly used in SMBs due to their intrinsic reductive stability. However, the solvated DME has lower lowest unoccupied molecular orbital (LUMO) compared that of pure DME, which causing the formation of a thick, high-resistance, and organic-rich SEI on sodium-metal anode (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202405803-fig-0001">1</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0002_R_d110631211e637" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Storage Mater. Nano Energy">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information), in 1 m NaFSI in DME electrolyte (hereafter denoted as 1-D), sodium-metal anode exhibits poor stability and a low CE (58.02%). Therefore, the 1-D electrolyte is not ideal for the high-Fermi-level sodium-metal anode. In order to improve the stability of SMBs, it is essential to design an electrolyte capable of deriving a more stable and inorganic-rich SEI film. Thus, the core design principle of electrolytes revolves around promoting more anions to enter the primary solvation sheath, enabling their preferential reduction at the anode surface to facilitate the formation of an inorganic-rich SEI.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405803-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/32c6d67b-f8d1-4e3e-8770-356c5ad09bdb/aenm202405803-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/32c6d67b-f8d1-4e3e-8770-356c5ad09bdb/aenm202405803-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a2860989-4cdf-4477-8269-77b5ace46fd9/aenm202405803-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/32c6d67b-f8d1-4e3e-8770-356c5ad09bdb/aenm202405803-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202405803-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405803" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematics of the solvation structures, interfacial reaction mechanisms, and Na deposition morphology in the conventional electrolytes, WSEs and the DWIEs.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In this context, THF, which exhibits weak binding energy with Na<sup>+</sup>, is chosen to replace the traditional DME for constructing WSEs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0025_R_d110631211e669" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information), Raman spectra confirm that, at the same concentration, the CIPs proportion increased from 25.62% of 1-D electrolyte to 76.24% of 1 m NaFSI in THF electrolyte (hereafter denoted as 1-T), which is attributed to the weaker binding energy of THF with Na<sup>+</sup>, thereby allowing more FSI<sup>−</sup> to enter the primary solvation sheath. According to the modified Aurbach method,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0026_R_d110631211e682" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Energy Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the CE of Na deposition/stripping is 87.34% in the 1-T electrolyte, which represents a significant improvement compared to that in 1-D electrolyte (58.02%). Clearly, constructing WSEs by using a lower solvation energy solvent is an effective approach to enhance the compatibility of the electrolyte with sodium-metal anode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405803-fig-0001">1</a>). However, the CE of 87.34% can't meet the reversibility requirements for the sodium-metal anode, significantly.</p>
            
            <p>To further reduce Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent interactions and enhance the proportion of CIPs and AGGs in the electrolyte, we have introduced DBE as a cosolvent in 1-T electrolyte. The DBE with longer alkyl chain has more pronounced steric hindrance effect, resulting in negligible solubility of NaFSI in DBE. Conversely, DBE shows excellent miscibility with THF. It is apparent that DBE can interact with THF, but it does not coordinate with Na<sup>+</sup>. By adjusting the concentration of NaFSI and the ratio of DBE to THF, the 1-T/B electrolyte achieves a delicate equilibrium of interactions among Na<sup>+</sup>, FSI<sup>−</sup>, THF, and DBE. The equilibrium state and the scope of the interactions of above components are discussed in Note <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). The results indicate that, when the volume ratio of DBE to THF is 1:1, the concentration of the dissolved NaFSI in the mixture solvent can reach 1 m, ultimately forming the target electrolyte of 1-T/B. The design concept of the target electrolyte, along with the SEI and Na deposition behavior derived from the solvation structure, is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405803-fig-0001">1</a>. Specifically, the introduced DBE, through its dipole interaction with THF, weakens the electronegativity of oxygen in THF, which further reduces the coordination between Na<sup>+</sup> and THF. This process may facilitate the formation of a double-layer solvation structure. Furthermore, the optimal solvation structure dominated by anions directly determines the formation of an inorganic-rich SEI. Ultimately, the stable and SEI film induce uniform and dense Na deposition, thereby improving the Na utilization efficiency. Moreover, in order to elucidate the function of DBE while eliminating the potential influence of localized high-concentration effects, we employed a 1.86 m NaFSI in THF electrolyte (hereafter denoted as 1.86-T) as a reference sample, because the NaFSI concentrations is 1.86 m based on THF and 1.77 m based on DBE within the 1-T/B electrolyte.</p>
            
            <p>The interaction schematic among the species in the 1-T/B electrolyte can be inferred and depicted in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202405803-fig-0002">2a</a>. To validate this design concept, Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) and Raman Spectroscopy techniques were employed to investigate the microstructure changes in the solution or electrolyte by the introduction of DBE. As observed in FTIR spectra, in the mixture of DBE and THF, the peak corresponding to C─O─C of the THF (≈1066 cm<sup>−1</sup>) shifts to higher wavenumbers (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405803-fig-0002">2b</a>). In contrast, the peak corresponding to C─O─C of the DBE (≈1120 cm<sup>−1</sup>) exhibits negligible variation, while the peak corresponding to C─H (≈962 cm<sup>−1</sup>) shifts to lower wavenumbers (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405803-fig-0002">2c</a>). These results directly reflect the dipole interactions between the O atoms in THF and the H atoms in DBE. Simultaneously, according to Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405803-fig-0002">2d</a>, upon the addition of NaFSI, the Raman vibration band corresponding to the C−O−C stretching vibration mode of the THF (900–930 cm<sup>−1</sup>) shifts to a higher wavenumber compared to that of the pure solvent, indicating the coordination of THF with Na<sup>+</sup>. With the concentration of NaFSI increasing, this peak of THF in 1.86-T electrolyte further shifts to a higher wavenumber, suggesting that more THF molecules participate in the Na<sup>+</sup> coordination. However, after furtherly introducing DBE, the mentioned peak shifts to a lower wavenumber, indicating that the dipole interactions between DBE and THF have weakened the interactions between THF and Na<sup>+</sup>. In addition, the FTIR spectra also display the same shifting trends of the C─O─C peaks of the THF (≈908 cm<sup>−1</sup> and ≈1066 cm<sup>−1</sup>) in 1-T, 1.86-T, and 1-T/B electrolytes with that of Raman spectra, which further prove the conclusions drawn above (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). Meanwhile, the 841 cm<sup>−1</sup> peak corresponding to the C─O─C stretching vibration mode of DBE (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405803-fig-0002">2d</a>) shows almost no shifting in the 1-T/B electrolyte compared with pure DBE, suggesting minimal interaction between DBE and Na<sup>+</sup>. This observation corresponds with the phenomenon described in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information), where NaFSI is nearly insoluble in DBE (1.77 m NaFSI in DBE denoted as 1.77-B). In summary, DBE does not directly participate in the formation of the primary solvation sheath in the solvation structure of the target electrolyte. Instead, it interacts with the main solvent (THF) through dipole interactions, ultimately forming a double-layer solvation structure.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405803-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/130044f6-746a-4e2e-8e01-357e4f949515/aenm202405803-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/130044f6-746a-4e2e-8e01-357e4f949515/aenm202405803-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/250f0b83-c5c4-496e-8992-717cd01a4258/aenm202405803-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/130044f6-746a-4e2e-8e01-357e4f949515/aenm202405803-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202405803-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405803" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The solvation structure of DWIEs. a) Schematic of interactions among Na<sup>+</sup>, FSI<sup>−</sup>, THF, and DBE within 1-T/B electrolyte. b,c) FTIR spectra of THF, DBE, and THF/DBE (1:1 by vol) mixed solvents. d) Raman spectra of 1-T, 1.86-T, and 1-T/B electrolytes and corresponding solvents. e) Fitting data of Raman spectra of different electrolytes at NaFSI regions. f) The proportion of SSIPs, CIPs, and AGGs in different electrolytes.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Furthermore, the solvation structure of 1-T, 1.86-T, and 1-T/B electrolytes were analyzed by Raman spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405803-fig-0002">2e</a>). The Raman vibration band in the range from 700 to 780 cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponds to the S−N−S stretching vibration mode of FSI<sup>−</sup> anions. This peak can be deconvoluted into three distinct solvation structures: solvent-separated ion pairs (SSIPs) (representing non-coordinated FSI<sup>−</sup>), CIPs (indicating one FSI<sup>−</sup> binding with one Na<sup>+</sup>), and AGGs (signifying one FSI<sup>−</sup> binding with two or more Na<sup>+</sup>), reflecting an increasing degree of FSI<sup>−</sup> participating in the solvation structure. The specific proportions of these three species were derived through peak area calculations and are listed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405803-fig-0002">2f</a>. The Na<sup>+</sup>-FSI<sup>−</sup> interactions increase in the order of 1-T &lt; 1.86-T &lt; 1-T/B electrolyte. From 1-T to 1.86-T electrolyte, as the NaFSI/THF molar ratio increases, more FSI<sup>−</sup> engaged in coordination with Na<sup>+</sup>, which leads to a rise in the overall proportion of CIPs and AGGs within the electrolyte, increasing from 89.18% to 93.08%. Moreover, the proportion of AGGs increases from 12.94% to 48.17%. In the 1-T/B electrolyte, FSI<sup>−</sup> further deeply engages in the solvation structure of Na<sup>+</sup> due to the weakened Na<sup>+</sup>-THF binding energy caused by the dipole interactions between DBE and THF. Specifically, the AGGs proportion increases to 62.4%. Consequently, it is fully demonstrated that the introduced DBE can assist in achieving an anion-dominated double-layer solvation structure through dipole interactions in the outer solvation layer.</p>
            
            <p>The optimization of the solvation structure has also led to changes in the physicochemical properties of the electrolyte. In terms of ionic conductivity, it is rising from 7.4 to 8.4 mS cm<sup>−1</sup> as the salt concentration increasing from 1.0 m to 1.86 m, which is attributed to the increased concentration of charge carriers (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). Upon the introduction of DBE, the ionic conductivity decreases to 4.4 mS cm<sup>−1</sup>. It is because the dipole interactions between DBE and THF reduce the dissociation ability of THF for NaFSI, resulting in a decrease in free ions. Nevertheless, it still ensures effective ion transport. Moreover, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information), the ESW of the 1-T electrolyte is ≈ 5.4 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. With the salt concentration increasing, the ESW of the 1.86-T electrolyte expands to ≈ 6.0 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. With the introduction of DBE, the ESW of the 1-T/B electrolyte further extends to ≈6.2 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. On the one hand, the potent electron-withdrawing effect of the long alkyl end chain results in its strong oxidation stability. On the other hand, the high ratio of Na<sup>+</sup> to THF and THF-DBE dipole interactions effectively reduce the activity of THF.</p>
            
            <p>In order to elucidate the influence of the solvation structure on the performance of the sodium-metal anode, the modified Aurbach method was used to evaluate the CE of Na deposition and stripping in Na||Cu cells. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202405803-fig-0003">3a</a>, the average CE in 1-T/B electrolyte (98.67%) is higher than that in 1.86-T (97.55%) and 1-T (87.34%). As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information), during the initial Na deposition stage, the overpotential profile of Na||Cu cell with 1-T electrolyte exhibits severe fluctuations, indicating the occurrence of soft short circuits and non-uniform Na deposition on the Cu foil.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0027_R_d110631211e862" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - ACS Cent. Sci.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, both 1.86-T and 1-T/B electrolytes exhibit smooth voltage profile without any spikes. Remarkably, the initial Na deposition/stripping CE in 1-T/B electrolyte increases by ≈13% compared to 1.86-T, implying that 1-T/B electrolyte establishes a more stable and efficient SEI. Additionally, the long-term cycling stability test of Na||Cu cells with these three electrolytes was performed. The results of Na||Cu cell testing as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405803-fig-0003">3b</a>, reveal that the initial CE (ICE) for the 1-T electrolyte is less than 50% (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a</a>, Supporting Information), and there are significant fluctuations in CE during the Na deposition and stripping processes. The 1.86-T electrolyte shows an ICE of 60% and achieves an average CE exceeding 92%. However, after running of 40 cycles, significant CE fluctuations start emerging (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9c</a>, Supporting Information), indicating that the non-uniform Na deposition and repeated SEI formation lead to continuous deterioration of the interface during the prolonged cycling process. Notably, the 1-T/B electrolyte shows a remarkable enhancement in ICE, nearly 90%, and the average CE remains consistently high at 98% over 100 cycles with almost no fluctuation during cycling. The observations are in good agreement with the results obtained by the modified Aurbach method. Since aluminum (Al) foil can serve as a substitute current collector for Cu foil in SIBs, the deposition and stripping behavior of Na on Al foil was investigated under the same test conditions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0028_R_d110631211e877" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Electrochem. Commun.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> According to Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10a</a> (Supporting Information), the voltage profile of Na deposition/stripping in the 1.86-T electrolyte fluctuates during the initial deposition process, implying a poor interface stability, and resulting in an average CE of only 88.8%, while the Na||Al cell with 1-T/B electrolyte maintaining a smooth Na deposition/stripping voltage profile and achieving an average CE exceeding 98%. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10b</a> (Supporting Information), the Na||Al cell with the 1-T/B electrolyte maintained an average CE of ≈97% over 50 cycles, with less fluctuation during cycling. The similar test results between the Na||Al and Na||Cu cells signify the exceptional SEI-forming capability of 1-T/B electrolyte, which plays a crucial role in the stable Na deposition and stripping.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405803-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d7408016-589f-4406-bf5a-954fd86fc0c4/aenm202405803-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/d7408016-589f-4406-bf5a-954fd86fc0c4/aenm202405803-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/766a30e7-c768-448e-be20-24b945788167/aenm202405803-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d7408016-589f-4406-bf5a-954fd86fc0c4/aenm202405803-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202405803-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405803" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Compatibility of 1-T, 1.86-T, and 1-T/B electrolytes with sodium-metal anode. a) CEs of Na||Cu cells by the modified Aurbach method. b) CEs of Na||Cu cells at a current density of 0.2 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> for 0.2 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. c) Cycling performance of Na||Na cells at a current density of 1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> for 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. SEM images of deposited Na on Cu foil in the d) 1.86-T and e) 1-T/B electrolytes. C 1s, O 1s, and F 1s XPS spectra of deposited Na on Cu foil in 1.86-T (f) and 1-T/B (g) electrolytes (first cycle deposition at 0.2 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> for 2.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>). The elemental composition and their proportion of SEI formed in (h) 1.86-T and (i) 1-T/B electrolytes.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Galvanostatic cycling measurements were conducted in Na||Na symmetric cells with current densities ranging from 0.5 to 4 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and cycling capacities from 0.5 to 4 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). Among the cells using above three electrolytes, the voltage profiles of the cell with 1-T electrolyte shows the largest overpotential and drastic spikes when the current density exceeds 1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12b</a>, Supporting Information). The abrupt decline in overpotential suggests the emergence of soft short circuits, indicating severe Na dendrite growth under high current densities.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0009_R_d110631211e938" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Chem. Commun. Chem. Eng. J. J. Power Sources ACS Appl. Energy Mater. ACS Energy Lett. ACS Energy Lett. J. Am. Chem. Soc.">9</a>, <a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0027_R_d110631211e938" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - ACS Cent. Sci.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The cell with 1.86-T electrolyte also exhibits higher overpotential across all current densities than that with 1-T/B, while the latter presents the most stable cycling with the smallest overpotential of ≈12 mV at 4 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12c</a>, Supporting Information). It is noteworthy that the contradiction between the low ionic conductivity and the low overpotential in 1-T/B electrolyte indicates that ion transport in bulk electrolyte is not the rate-determined step in the Na deposition/stripping process, which may be related to the ion transport in SEI film. Moreover, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405803-fig-0003">3c</a> displays the cycling performance of Na||Na symmetric cells (50 µm thickness) with three electrolytes at a current density of 1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and a cycling capacity of 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. The overpotential of Na||Na cell with 1-T electrolyte starts fluctuating at ≈ 80 h, and subsequently shows large spikes, leading to a quick failure of the cell (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13a</a>, Supporting Information). As for 1.86-T electrolyte, although the overpotential maintains stable at latter cycling, there is a noticeable overpotential fluctuation after pre-cycling at a current density of 0.2 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, indicating the continuous thickening of the SEI film, which in turn leads to an increase in impedance. In contrast, the cell with 1-T/B electrolyte shows stable voltage profiles with lowest overpotential (&lt;10 mV) at all stage of cycling (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13b,c</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>The interfacial chemistry is crucial for achieving both good Na reversibility and compatibility between the electrolyte and the sodium-metal anode. First, the Cyclic Voltammetry (CV) results of Na||Cu cells with different electrolytes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information) further revealed well SEI formation ability of 1-T/B electrolyte. Specifically, in the first CV discharge curve of the Na||Cu cell with the 1-T/B electrolyte, a distinct anion decomposition peak is observed at 0.5 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>, which is not present in the other two electrolytes. And the current stabilized within 5 cycles, demonstrating the stability of the anion-derived SEI. Meanwhile, the EIS results of Na||Na symmetric cells (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information) indicate that the charge transfer impedance of the cell with 1-T/B electrolyte after cycling is considerably lower, which confirms that the SEI formed by the DWIE promotes Na<sup>+</sup> transport. The CV tests and post-cycling EIS results collectively provide kinetic evidences supporting our design concept. Subsequently, Na deposition morphology on Cu foil was observed by scanning electron microscopy (SEM). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405803-fig-0003">3d</a>, despite the solvation structure of the 1.86-T electrolyte being optimized compared to that of the conventional electrolyte, the morphology of Na deposition in it is still loose, with numerous pores and Na dendrites. In contrast, the double-layer solvation structure of the 1-T/B electrolyte results in dense and uniform Na deposition (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405803-fig-0003">3e</a>). As we all know, SEI is affected by the solvation structure, which directly influences the Na deposition morphology. Thereby, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was used to characterize the components of the SEI formed in 1.86-T and 1-T/B electrolytes, respectively. According to Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405803-fig-0003">3f,g</a>, the components containing C and O elements of the SEI generated from above two electrolytes are similar, which indicates that the solvents are still decomposing at the interface and participating in the formation of the organic components of the SEI. However, compared to that from 1.86-T, the components of SEI derived from 1-T/B electrolyte exhibit higher content of F and N elements. Given the non-fluorinated solvents, the inorganic components of the SEI primarily originate from anion decomposition. The stronger S-F and NaF signals in the F 2s spectra, along with the appearance of the SO<sub>2</sub>-F signal in the S 2p spectra, further confirm that an inorganic-rich SEI is formed by the 1-T/B electrolyte (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0029_R_d110631211e992" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Nat. Commun.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the higher intensity of S<sub>X</sub><sup>2−</sup> in S 2p spectra from 1-T/B electrolyte signifies a more complete reduction of FSI<sup>−</sup> within the electrolyte. A stable and inert SEI can inhibit side reactions between the electrolyte and the sodium-metal anode, reducing the consumption of the electrolyte and regulating the Na deposition behavior to retain the reversible Na inventory at the interface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0030_R_d110631211e1003" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Energy Storage Mater. Nat. Energy">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> And the inorganic components within the SEI can accelerate the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion, meanwhile facilitating stable Na deposition/stripping and enabling a long-cycling lifespan. The comprehensive analysis and comparison of Na deposition morphology and SEI components firmly substantiate the effectiveness of the dual-weak-interaction design strategy.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405803-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202405803-sec-0040-title">2.2 Regulation of Dipole Interaction of DWIEs</h3>
            
            <p>Obviously, DWIE relies on the dipole interactions between main solvent and cosolvent. Therefore, enhancing dipole interactions can further optimize the anode compatibility of the electrolyte, theoretically. To this end, we further regulated the molecular configuration of the cosolvent to optimize the second weak interaction effect. Specifically, we chose DBEi, the isomer of DBE, to investigate how cosolvent configurations affect solvation structure and weakly solvating effects. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202405803-fig-0004">4a</a>, the configuration difference of butyl connecting ether functional groups results in different electrostatic potential (ESP) distributions between the two molecules, which will inevitably lead to a difference in the dipole interaction between it and the main solvent. As depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405803-fig-0004">4b</a>, the ether oxygen characteristic peak shifts for THF (≈1066 cm<sup>−1</sup>) and DBEi (≈1114 cm<sup>−1</sup>) of the THF/DBEi mixture in the FTIR spectra are more significant than those of the THF/DBE mixture. This suggests a stronger dipole interaction between THF and DBEi, which further weakens the Na<sup>+</sup>-THF interactions and promotes more anions to enter the primary solvation sheath of Na<sup>+</sup>. According to Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), DBE and DBEi serve similar functions in DWIEs, where they restrict the solubility of the electrolyte and compete with cations for coordination with THF, thus facilitating the formation of an anion-dominated solvation structure. Additionally, the higher binding energy of THF-DBEi (-0.0032 eV) compared to that of THF-DBE (-0.0016 eV) further confirms the above conclusion (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information). According to the radial distribution function (RDF) analysis, when the distance is less than 5 Å, the coordination probability of DBE or DBEi is almost close to 0 in the 1-T/B and 1-T/Bi electrolytes, indicating most of cosolvents are not involved in the primary solvation sheath of Na<sup>+</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405803-fig-0004">4c,d</a>). Noteworthily, in the primary solvation sheath at ≈ 2 Å, both the coordination probability ratio of FSI<sup>−</sup> to THF around Na<sup>+</sup> and the FSI<sup>−</sup> coordination number in 1-T/Bi are higher than those in 1-T/B, which further demonstrates that the optimization of the cosolvent molecular configuration is more conducive to the participation of anions in the main solvation structure.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405803-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/56cade4d-ac18-4257-a67a-398c9169d887/aenm202405803-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/56cade4d-ac18-4257-a67a-398c9169d887/aenm202405803-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/00b307f2-5e68-4400-aad5-0ce9d2c263a4/aenm202405803-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/56cade4d-ac18-4257-a67a-398c9169d887/aenm202405803-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202405803-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405803" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Regulation of dual-weak-interaction effects. a) ESP of of DBE and DBEi. b) FTIR spectra of THF/DBE and THF/DBEi mixed solvents (1:1 by vol). The RDF profile of c) 1-T/Bi and d) 1-T/B electrolytes. e) CEs of Na||Cu cells by the modified Aurbach method at a current density of 0.4 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>. f) Long-term cycling and CEs of Na||Cu cells at a current density of 0.2 for 0.2 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Then, we investigated the compatibility of 1-T/B and 1-T/Bi electrolytes with the sodium-metal anode. According to the modified Aurbach method (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405803-fig-0004">4e</a>), at the current density of 0.4 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, the CE of 1-T/Bi electrolyte reaches 99.22%, outperforming that of 1-T/B (98.56%). Meanwhile, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> (Supporting Information) shows that the ICE of 1-T/Bi electrolyte reaches 97.19%, which is also significantly higher than that of 1-T/B (93.12%). In addition, the results from the long-term cycling of Na||Cu cells corroborate those from Aurbach method. As displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405803-fig-0004">4f</a>, at the current density of 0.2 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> for 0.2 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, the Na||Cu half cell with 1-T/Bi electrolyte shows an initial CE of 93.99% (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information) and exhibits ultra-stable cycling with an average CE of 99.05% for nearly 500 cycles. In contrast, the Na||Cu cell with 1-T/B electrolyte could only maintain stable cycling for ≈ 150 cycles. At a current density of 0.5 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, the 1-T/Bi electrolyte can also maintain an average CE of 98.74% over 100 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information). Additionally, according to the SEM images (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information), it can be observed that after cycling in the 1-T/B electrolyte, the Na deposition morphology on the Na||Cu cell is relatively smooth with almost no dendrite growth. However, small cracks can still be observed on the surface. In comparison, within the 1-T/Bi electrolyte, the Na deposition morphology is much smoother and more compact, with almost no cracks observed. Moreover, the anion decomposition peak in the first cycle CV discharge curves (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information) of the 1-T/Bi electrolyte occurs at a higher potential than that of 1-T/B. The smaller interface impedance (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information) means the faster ionic transport rate within SEI film. Both of them confirm that the 1-T/Bi electrolyte can form an SEI film with lower impedance. These results indicate that the introduction of DBEi can achieve a significant weakly solvating effect and improve the stability of the electrolyte against the sodium-metal anode, which also confirms the universality of the dual-weak-interaction design strategy.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405803-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202405803-sec-0050-title">2.3 Practical Performance of SMBs with DWIEs</h3>
            
            <p>Lastly, the electrochemical performances of NVP||Na cells with three electrolytes were comparatively investigated. When using the high NVP loading of ≈8.8 mg cm<sup>−2</sup> to match 50 µm thickness Na (N/P ratio is 6), the SMB using 1-T/Bi electrolyte shows prolonged cycling stability compared to that using 1-T/B and 1.86-T electrolytes. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202405803-fig-0005">5a,b</a>, the full-cell with 1.86-T electrolyte can only maintain stable cycling for 40 cycles under such stringent conditions. At the 42<sup>th</sup> cycle, the charging and discharging curves exhibit severe fluctuations and voltage hysteresis and without any capacity in subsequent cycles. This suggests that the sodium-metal anode is nearly depleted after 40 cycles, leading to a lack of reversible Na storage, which can be attributed to the poor Na deposition/stripping efficiency.Under the same harsh conditions, the cell with 1-T/B electrolyte can maintain 170 stable cycles and retain 94.65% of its initial capacity. Moreover, the cell using 1-T/Bi electrolyte shows prolonged lifespan of 217 cycles with 93.58% of its initial capacity retained, which is five times than that with 1.86-T electrolyte. Notably, for all cells using different electrolytes, the charge/discharge plateaus show increased polarization in the later stages of cycling. After an increase in polarization, the charge and discharge capacity sharply declines with subsequent cycles, ultimately resulting in cell failure. The underlying cause of this phenomenon is the depletion of reversible Na. First, under limited Na conditions, the reversible Na is gradually consumed with each cycle. During the later stages of cycling process, the significant consumption in available Na directly decreases the Na<sup>+</sup> concentration in the electrolyte, thereby droping the ionic conductivity, resulting in the polarization increase for the charge/discharge voltage plateaus. Second, during extended cycling, the accumulation of inert products at the anode interface leads to the formation of an excessively thick SEI layer, which increases the ion transport distance and consequently exacerbates polarization. This leads to an increase in polarization and accelerates the failure of the cell.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405803-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/85d88549-83b9-47f5-9803-644f3b4323a0/aenm202405803-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/85d88549-83b9-47f5-9803-644f3b4323a0/aenm202405803-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/18723c66-5efe-49b2-a644-6fc471d81e66/aenm202405803-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/85d88549-83b9-47f5-9803-644f3b4323a0/aenm202405803-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202405803-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405803" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of NVP||Na cells with different electrolytes. a) The charge-discharge curves of NVP||Na cells toward different electrolytes. b) Cycling performance of high-loading NVP (8.8 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>) ||Na (50 µm) full-cells with different electrolytes at 1C. c) Long-term cycling performance of NVP||Na cell with different electrolytes at 5C. d) A summary of CEs for Na||Cu cells tested using the Aurbach method based on electrolyte design. More details are given in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0031" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0031_R_d110631211e1162" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Energy Chem.">31</a>-<a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0041" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0041_R_d110631211e1162" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> e) Comparison of the enhancement in cycling performance of NVP||Na full-cells and CE of Na||Cu cells achieved by solvation structure optimization.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>When using 400 µm thickness Na, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405803-fig-0005">5c</a>, the NVP||Na cell using 1-T/Bi electrolyte exhibits enduring cycling for over 3000 cycles at 5C, maintaining 91.71% of its initial capacity. Meanwhile, the cell with 1-T/B electrolyte maintains stable cycling for nearly 1000 cycles and retains 94.02% of its initial capacity. In contrast, the cell with 1.86-T electrolyte fails within 300 cycles. Additionally, we evaluated the rate performance of NVP||Na cells using different electrolytes, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a> (Supporting Information). Although the ionic conductivity of DWIEs is low, thanks to the balance of the low impedance SEI film, NVP||Na cell using DWIEs still maintains 102 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> discharge capacity at a high rate of 10C, which is even slightly higher than the performance of the cells with other electrolytes. The performance improvement reflects the excellent utilization of sodium-metal anode enabled by the dual-weak-interaction design strategy, confirming its substantial advantages in practical SMB applications.</p>
            
            <p>According to the comparison in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405803-fig-0005">5d,e</a>, the Na||Cu cells using DWIEs exhibit outstanding CE, outperforming most other electrolyte design strategies (e.g., carbonate electrolytes, additives, partial LHCEs, and common WSEs). The specific statistics are listed in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0031_R_d110631211e1187" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Energy Chem.">31</a>-<a href="#aenm202405803-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405803-bib-0041_R_d110631211e1187" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the meantime, the enhanced cycling performance of the NVP||Na full-cells, coupled with the significant improvement in CE of the Na||Cu cells, highlights that the target electrolyte, with its optimized solvation structure, has achieved excellent compatibility with the sodium-metal anode, significantly enhancing its utilization efficiency.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202405803-sec-0060">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202405803-sec-0060-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we proposed a dual-weak-interaction design strategy and developed a novel DWIE with excellent anode compatibility for SMBs. By selecting the weak Na<sup>+</sup>-coordinating main solvent THF and introducing dipole interactions between the cosolvent DBE and the THF, the binding energy between Na<sup>+</sup> and THF is further weakened, thereby promoting more FSI<sup>−</sup> to coordinate with Na<sup>+</sup>, achieving a CIPs and AGGs dominant solvation structure at low salt concentration. Moreover, the dipole interaction effect can be further strengthened by regulating the molecular configuration of cosolvent, specifically by replacing DBE with DBEi. Benefiting from the anion-derived inorganic-rich SEI, the CEs of Na||Cu cells with 1-T/B and 1-T/Bi electrolytes achieve significant enhancements (with average CEs reaching 98.56% and 99.22%, respectively) and exhibit impressive stability (with average CEs reaching 98% for 1-T/B over 100 cycles and 99.05% for 1-T/Bi over nearly 500 cycles). The enhanced compatibility of the 1-T/Bi electrolyte with sodium-metal anode results in the practical SMB achieving a cycling lifespan of 217 cycles, leading to a fivefold extension in comparison to that using the single-solvent electrolyte (1.86-T). Moreover, at 5C, the NVP||Na cell using 1-T/Bi electrolyte exhibits prolonged stable cycling for over 3000 cycles, tripling the cycling lifespan of that using 1-T/B electrolyte. In addition, with low salt concentration and low cost of the fluorine-free cosolvents, the cost-effectiveness and eco-friendliness of the electrolytes are underscored even more prominently. This work pioneers a novel perspective and provides a universal method toward realizing ultra-weak solvation strategy, and holds promise for applications in other battery systems.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
